    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12095890648770461730"><b>'Holmes,' I cried. 'Is it really you? Can it indeed be that you are alive? Is it possible that you succeeded in climbing out of that awful abyss?'</b><br/><br/>Missing, presumed dead, for three years, Sherlock Holmes returns triumphantly to his dear companion Dr Watson. And not before time! London has never been in more need of his extraordinary services: a murderous individual with an </span>
  <span id="freeText12095890648770461730" style="display:none"><b>'Holmes,' I cried. 'Is it really you? Can it indeed be that you are alive? Is it possible that you succeeded in climbing out of that awful abyss?'</b><br /><br />Missing, presumed dead, for three years, Sherlock Holmes returns triumphantly to his dear companion Dr Watson. And not before time! London has never been in more need of his extraordinary services: a murderous individual with an air gun stalks the city.<br /><br />Among thirteen further brilliant tales of mystery, detection and deduction, Sherlock Holmes investigates the problem of the Norwood Builder, deciphers the message of the Dancing Men, and cracks the case of the Six Napoleons.</span>

      </div>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_506064').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});

          });

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22106879-jayson?shelf=400-499-pp">400-499-pp</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22106879-jayson?shelf=genre-detective">genre-detective</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer683649205" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13794628038625405336"><b>(A) 85%</b> | Extraordinary<br/><i>Notes:</i> All in all, a volume without a real stand-out story, but the most consistently good of the short story collections so far.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2782859996" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2782859996" />
    <a title="Werner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/903390-werner"><img alt="Werner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216337931p2/903390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2782859996?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Werner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Werner" href="/user/show/903390-werner">Werner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of short format, traditional mysteries</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2782859996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7018777247262853595">June 29, 2019<br/>Collecting 13 Sherlock Holmes tales which originally appeared in <i>The Strand</i> magazine from Oct. 1903 to Jan. 1905, and published in book form in 1905, this is the third of Doyle's five story collections featuring Holmes, the second one being the one I recently reviewed, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/194373.The_Memoirs_of_Sherlock_Holmes__Sherlock_Holmes___4_' title='The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes (Sherlock Holmes, #4) by Arthur Conan Doyle' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes</a>. Indeed, the lead story here, "The Adventure of the Empty House," is essentially a sequel to the last story in the previous collection, "The Adventure of the Final Problem." (In all of t</span>
  <span id="freeText7018777247262853595" style="display:none">June 29, 2019<br />Collecting 13 Sherlock Holmes tales which originally appeared in <i>The Strand</i> magazine from Oct. 1903 to Jan. 1905, and published in book form in 1905, this is the third of Doyle's five story collections featuring Holmes, the second one being the one I recently reviewed, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/194373.The_Memoirs_of_Sherlock_Holmes__Sherlock_Holmes___4_" title="The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes (Sherlock Holmes, #4) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes</a>. Indeed, the lead story here, "The Adventure of the Empty House," is essentially a sequel to the last story in the previous collection, "The Adventure of the Final Problem." (In all of these collections, the arrangement of the stories is apparently in the order they were written.) The former story is one of two here that I'd already read; the other is "The Adventure of Charles Augustus Milverton," which is one of my favorite stories in the Holmes canon, though hard to discuss without a spoiler. (It was adapted for the PBS <i>Mystery!</i> TV series, starring Jeremy Brett, as "The Master Blackmailer;" I can recommend that version, but it does flesh out the original with significant added material.)<br /><br />From what I've read so far, I would say that stylistically, the stories here are typical of the Holmes corpus --which is to say, very readable, flowing well, with appropriate levels of description and character development, posing challenging intellectual puzzles that call forth satisfying displays of deduction, often with atmospheric and Gothic touches, and with inherent emotional impact and sometimes thought-provoking situations. Holmes' character dominates the book, and he's definitely one of the genre's most individual and memorable sleuths. (One reviewer did mention a "a heavy reliance on 'someone's past comes back to haunt them'" and I'd agree that this is a theme that crops up in several Holmes stories, here and elsewhere.)<br /><br />While waiting for a common read in one of my groups, I read five of the stories here. I guessed the main outlines of the solution in both "The Adventure of the Norwood Builder" and "The Adventure of the Solitary Cyclist," but it was still fun to see Holmes demonstrate and prove it, and both stories held some definite surprises nonetheless. Similarly, "The Adventure of the Priory School" was another story where I'd seen the PBS adaptation, so again I knew the basic solution; but the story differed from the adaptation in some particulars, and I'd forgotten others, so it wasn't a spoiled read on that account. "The Adventure of the Dancing Men" allowed Holmes to demonstrate his code-breaking skills to advantage. Finally, "The Adventure of Black Peter," provided one of Holmes' grislier murder cases, with the demise of a generally despised retired sea captain, his body found pinned to a wall with one of his own harpoons. Will read more later!<br /><br />Aug. 24, 2019<br />Yesterday, I finished reading the final six of these stories; and I'm increasingly suspecting I've read this collection, and at least some of the other later Holmes collections as well, as a kid, since I had the experience (not for the first time, in reading these books!) of recognizing a passage of dialogue I'd read before. I definitely recall that my reading of <i>The Adventures of Sherlock Holmes</i>, back in the mists of childhood, set me to hunting up and reading every Sherlock Holmes book I could find. Only the titles of the first collection and the four novels stayed in my memory; but given my tendency to forget title information for books I read in those days, it's quite likely I read all, or at least much more, of the canon at that age than I specifically remembered! However, I'd forgotten all of the other details of that missing-person story, "The Adventure of the Missing Three Quarter" (the title comes from the terminology of the British sport of cricket --a world as new to Holmes as to me), so it and all of the other stories were as good as new reads. (I'd seen a TV adaptation of "The Adventure of the Golden Pince-Nez," on <i>Mystery!</i> years ago, which has two of Doyle's more memorable characters, and I recalled those two individuals, but not the details of the mystery and its solution.)<br /><br />My favorite story in this batch was "The Adventure of the Abbey Grange," which is also one of Holmes' grislier murder cases --though its grisliness wasn't the reason I liked it! That one is marred in one place by a character's use of "white man" as a complimentary term synonymous with "man of personal integrity," which is obviously racist; but Doyle is simply reproducing there the kind of speech that a character with these characteristics and background might well have used in that era. (The same usage rears its head, for instance, in John Buchan's <i>The 39 Steps</i>, which dates from the same period.) One story in <i>The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes</i> actually makes a very good statement about racial issues and is refreshingly free of the usual British racism of that day; to avoid a "spoiler," I won't identify the story, but it probably reflects the author's own attitudes more reliably than a casual comment put in the mouth of a character who appears once.<br /><br />In reading "The Adventure of the Six Napoleons," I deduced the basic premise behind the solution almost immediately, but again it was still fun watching Holmes work it out and fill in the details. The other mysteries were more difficult to resolve until the denouement, though in a couple of cases I'd formed a basic theory along the way which was correct as far as it went. "The Adventure of the Second Stain" takes Holmes and Watson, once again, into the world of high stakes international espionage. My main take-away from "The Adventure of the Three Students" is that it reflects a mind-set of English (and probably American as well) college students in an era that still saw academic cheating as something beneath an honorable person --and indeed, still felt that a person <i>could</i> be honorable, and that this would actually be a good thing. (Attitudes have changed today, and not for the better; but having read this soon after being present for the signing, at the start of another school year, of the Bluefield College Honor Code by the incoming freshmen, I'm glad that I'm serving an institution that chooses to go against the grain in that respect!)<br /><br />Like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/194373.The_Memoirs_of_Sherlock_Holmes__Sherlock_Holmes___4_" title="The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes (Sherlock Holmes, #4) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes</a>, which I reviewed earlier, I read this collection (this time, at least) as part of the omnibus volume <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/76234.The_Original_Illustrated_Sherlock_Holmes" title="The Original Illustrated Sherlock Holmes by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Original Illustrated Sherlock Holmes</a>, which reproduces all of the illustrations of the stories done by Sidney Paget when they were first published in <i>The Strand</i>. These enhance the read, IMO (kids aren't the only readers who can enjoy illustrations; we adults do, too! :-) )</span>
  <a data-text-id="7018777247262853595" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2782859996">

<div id="review_2914125741" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2914125741" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2914125741?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=brittish-lit">brittish-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=classic-mystery">classic-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2914125741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4686412476213070814">Sherlock Holmes has not died after all! :) That was a very happy development. I was hoping he would somehow return (from the dead) given the title of this collection. At the same time I also entertained the idea that perhaps this collection refers to the cases before his supposed death. All was cleared in <i> The Adventure of the Empty House </i>, when Holmes makes a dramatic re-entry scaring the poor Doctor Watson out of his wits. <br/><br/>With his return, Sherlock Holmes brings to us (through Dr. Watson's chr</span>
  <span id="freeText4686412476213070814" style="display:none">Sherlock Holmes has not died after all! :) That was a very happy development. I was hoping he would somehow return (from the dead) given the title of this collection. At the same time I also entertained the idea that perhaps this collection refers to the cases before his supposed death. All was cleared in <i> The Adventure of the Empty House </i>, when Holmes makes a dramatic re-entry scaring the poor Doctor Watson out of his wits. <br /><br />With his return, Sherlock Holmes brings to us (through Dr. Watson's chronicles of course) thirteen interesting adventures of his where, as always, his brilliant, analytical mind and somewhat supernatural faculties as well as his power of deduction is demonstrated to his great advantage. This is the third collection of short stories that I read of the adventures of Sherlock Holmes and I found it to be the most interesting to me. Except for a couple, all the others were generously rated between four and five stars: <b> The Adventure of the Empty House </b> *****, <b> The Adventure of the Norwood Builder </b> *****, <b> The Adventure of the Dancing Men </b> *****, <b> The Adventure of the Solitary Cyclist </b> *****, <b> The Adventure of the Six Napoleons </b> *****, <b> The Adventure of the Missing Three-Quarter </b> *****, <b> The Adventure of the Abbey Grange </b> *****, <b> The Adventure of the Priory School </b> ****, <b> The Adventure of Charles Augustus Milverton </b> ****, <b> The Adventure of the Three Students </b> ****, <b> The Adventure of the Golden Pince-Nez </b> ****, <b> The Adventure of the Second Stain </b> *** and <b> The Adventure of Black Peter </b> ***. <br /><br />The eccentricity and arrogance were two marked characteristics of Sherlock Holmes. They were all the more marked in these stories, especially his arrogance. Although I was annoyed at times when his arrogance was directed towards Doctor Watson, I was able to overlook this weakness enough to respect and admire the eccentric detective. The only other complain I have is the fact that Conan Doyle has reduced Doctor Watson to a mere chronicler and a secretary. I wished the author had assigned a more useful role to the worthy doctor. <br /><br />With this read, I have completed the major works of Sherlock Holmes series. I'm really glad that Conan Doyle bowed down to the popular opinion and decided to continue the series long after his erroneous decision to mark the end of the series with <i> The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes </i>. We readers would otherwise have been deprived of some of the best adventures of Sherlock Holmes - the best fictitious detective ever to grace the literary world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4686412476213070814" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2448280350" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2448280350" />
    <a title="Aishu Rehman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73221135-aishu-rehman"><img alt="Aishu Rehman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546532143p2/73221135.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2448280350?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aishu Rehman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aishu Rehman" href="/user/show/73221135-aishu-rehman">Aishu Rehman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73221135-aishu-rehman?shelf=ar-conan-doyle">ar-conan-doyle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73221135-aishu-rehman?shelf=english-literature">english-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2448280350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6058345139440132459">The Return of Sherlock Holmes collects thirteen short stories featuring the famous detective, all supposedly written and published by John Watson, his best friend, roommate, and confidante. (With a disappearing wife.) After the events of “The Adventure of the Final Problem”, Holmes returns, in dramatic fashion, and the two set out to solve crimes all over London, from theft to blackmail to international espionage.<br/><br/>While I have plenty more to say about the “I Believe in Sherlock” movement (I may h</span>
  <span id="freeText6058345139440132459" style="display:none">The Return of Sherlock Holmes collects thirteen short stories featuring the famous detective, all supposedly written and published by John Watson, his best friend, roommate, and confidante. (With a disappearing wife.) After the events of “The Adventure of the Final Problem”, Holmes returns, in dramatic fashion, and the two set out to solve crimes all over London, from theft to blackmail to international espionage.<br /><br />While I have plenty more to say about the “I Believe in Sherlock” movement (I may have participated?), I’ll just say here that it amazes and humbles me that Holmes’ “death” has always affected fans to the point of action. And I was wondering how Doyle would actually bring him back. “The Adventure of the Empty House” does not disappoint. While the stories are ultimately adventure pieces, Holmes and Watson’s reunion is actually touching and the two do communicate how much they mean to each other—in fact, Holmes underestimated the affect his disappearance would have on Watson. They fall back into their familiar rhythm over the course of the story; they’re even a little handsy with each other as they readjust. I mean, more than usual. And at the end, all returns to status quo. (Holmes buys Watson’s practice in a roundabout manner and Mary seems to have vanished into thin air, so now they’re just living solely on Holmes’ paycheck, I assume. Oh, boys.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="6058345139440132459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_348626022" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/348626022" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/348626022?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1662951-apokripos">Apokripos</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=detective">detective</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer348626022" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3839605799581267322">I know now why this collection of 13 short stories is called “The Return..” In the last story of “Memoirs” entitled <i>The Final Problem,</i> Watson says that Holmes has retired and will no longer publish new stories. Now in real life, between that story which was published in 1893 and 1903, the period called by Sherlock fans as “The Great Hiatus,” Sir Arthur Conan Doyle only wrote his third novel, <i>The Hound of the Baskervilles</i> (said to be his greatest novel). So, while writing the first story of the c</span>
  <span id="freeText3839605799581267322" style="display:none">I know now why this collection of 13 short stories is called “The Return..” In the last story of “Memoirs” entitled <i>The Final Problem,</i> Watson says that Holmes has retired and will no longer publish new stories. Now in real life, between that story which was published in 1893 and 1903, the period called by Sherlock fans as “The Great Hiatus,” Sir Arthur Conan Doyle only wrote his third novel, <i>The Hound of the Baskervilles</i> (said to be his greatest novel). So, while writing the first story of the collection, Doyle was in full hope that the interest in his characters, Holmes and Watson, would be revived. I actually rested for few days in between reading the two collections and I would say that on the 6th day, I felt that I was missing Sherlock Holmes so I returned to my daily reading and finished this book in 4 days including two heavy weekend reading days: <br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Empty House</b> tells that Holmes has survived the fall from the Reinchenbach Falls with his archenemy Professor Mortiary. However, one of Mortiary’s confederates, Moran, knows about this so Holmes hides for three years and comes back in disguise. Watson, now a widower, recognizes him and so they are both back to resolving crimes in London. Their first assignment is the murder of Ronald Adair in Park Lane and the culprit is no other than his card playmate, who is but Moran. <i>Very enjoyable after almost a week of not reading Sherlock Holmes. My friend is correct in advising me of not reading this canon without letup. Holmes almost convinced me went abroad to see the Dalai Lama!</i> – 4 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Norwood Builder</b> is about Oldacre who wants to start a new life so he fakes his murder and sets up the son of the woman who has ditched him. Holmes does the unorthodox solution: fakes a fire and let the 3 cohorts to shout “Fire!” Oldacre comes out from the hiding. <i>Also enjoyable but I could not believe that Oldacre did not know that the fire was staged. </i> - 3 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Dancing Men</b> is about the death of a couple, American lady called Elsie and the British guy called Cubitt. Prior to their deaths, the images of the 15 Dancing Men mysteriously appear in their house. <i>Although I loved the idea, I just can’t imagine how the ditched American lover was able to draw them on the wall without being caught. Oh, also what happened to the fingerprints on the guns used?</i> - 2 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Solitary Cyclist</b> Violet is seeing a man on a bicycle following her. It seems to have something to do with the uncle who contacts Carruthers and Woodley to ensure the well-being of Violet and her mother. <i>Quite ordinary. I thought it would have been more interesting if the love story is highlighted more. But then this is Sherlock Holmes and not Nicholas Sparks. </i> - 2 STARS<br /> <br /><b>The Adventure of the Priory School</b> Lord Saltire is missing from Priory School. Apparently, he leaves the school and is chased by Heidegger, the school’s master. The boy is reported to be unhappy at home and it is assumed to be the reason. Holmes and Watson deduced by first looking at the cow’s tracks and noticing that one of the bicycles has a patch. <i>I liked this one because it has a second set of characters that come out during the deduction part that I did not expect coming.</i> - 3 STARS<br /> <br /><b>The Adventure of Black Peter</b> A whaler father is found dead, murdered in his store. His family is happy because he, abusive to them, is finally out of their lives. Investigator Hopkins asks for help from Holmes and Watson. Holmes sees the initial on the cover of the diary in the crime scene and it leads him to the culprit. <i>Seems ordinary to me.</i> - 2 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of Charles Augustus Milverton</b> The character of Charles Augustus Milverton is based on the life of Charles Augustus Howell (1840-1890) who persuaded the poet Dante Gabriel Rossetti to dig the up the poems he buried with his wife Elizabeth Siddal. He was said to have used those letters to bribe well-known personalities. Sir Conan Doyle used this true-to-life situation as a backdrop of this story. Here, Holmes is hired by a debutante Lady Eva Blackwell to retrieve compromising letters from a blackmailer, Milverton. Holmes disguises as a plumber, applies in the Blackwell mansion and get engaged with one of the maids. <i>Conan Doyle has limitless imagination and can make Holmes do everything especially all these almost unbelievable disguises.</i> - 4 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Six Napoleons</b> The busts of Napoleon are being shattered night after night by an unknown person. Through the power of Holmes’ deduction he found out why. <i>Direct storytelling. I thought it had something to do with British hating Napoleon, a French military commander and conquerer. Until the revelation. </i> - 3 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Three Students</b> Three students are suspected to have “prototype” of the exams that would qualify them for a big scholarship. The incident happens when the professor is out from his office. Holmes is consulted to do a mini-mini-minimo. <i>I liked this because it is simple and brought back memories of classmates who were caught cheating during exams in school. Not that I did not think of committing similar acts! </i> - 4 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Golden Pince-Nez</b> The Golden Pince-Nez is a pair of eyeglasses that gets lost during an accident killing of Willoughby Smith, secretary to Professor Coram. Check the back of the bookcase. <i>This story is short and quite uncomplicated compared to the other stories but I enjoyed it!</i> - 3 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Missing Three-Quarter</b> Very sad discovery where Staunton is. I thought that this story is sport-related until the ending. <i> Quite surprisingly, I am liking the Holmes stories with light straightforward resolutions.</i> - 3 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Abbey Grange</b> An abusive husband, Sir Eustace Brackenstall is murdered while his wife for 18 months, Lady Brackenstall is tied on a post. The lady and her maid say that the culprit is a group of 3 burglars. The answer is obvious. <i> I knew it! I was able to predict this one!</i> - 2 STARS<br /><br /><b>The Adventure of the Second Stain</b> A missing document from the dispatch box of the Secretary of State, Lord Bellinger. He believes that no one knows the importance of that document even his wife, Lady Bellinger. Then one of the spies hired by Holmes is killed. <i>Tight plot. I did not see the ending coming but I again anticipated the marital problem. </i> - 3 STARS<br /><br />Now my reading of the short stories about Sherlock Holmes is becoming interesting. There is a formula: crime  initial investigation  seems lame/untrue  investigate  first clues  deduction  new characters/setting/real events  conclusion. So, while reading, I can tell where I am. Then if I can predict what happens in the last two stages, it makes me happy. The only thing that I should improve on is how to do deductions! I have not been able to think the way Holmes does! He is just too smart!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3839605799581267322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241952955" href="/book/show/12483556-the-return-of-sherlock-holmes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=public-library">public-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2019">read-in-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=summer-of-sherlock">summer-of-sherlock</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2888264414" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14253946449187578317"><b>
  <i>***The Summer of Sherlock 2019***</i>
</b><br/><br/>Sir Arthur Conan Doyle may have been reluctant to raise Sherlock Holmes from the dead, but he certainly provided some entertaining stories after his sudden return.<br/><br/>I confess that I was quite chuffed when I had figured out what was going on in <i>The Adventure of the Six Napoleons</i> before the great detective was ready to reveal the motivation of the criminal. And I still have some nagging memories concerning <i>The Adventure of Charles Augustus Milverton</i>, as I am sure</span>
  <span id="freeText14253946449187578317" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>***The Summer of Sherlock 2019***</i>
</b><br /><br />Sir Arthur Conan Doyle may have been reluctant to raise Sherlock Holmes from the dead, but he certainly provided some entertaining stories after his sudden return.<br /><br />I confess that I was quite chuffed when I had figured out what was going on in <i>The Adventure of the Six Napoleons</i> before the great detective was ready to reveal the motivation of the criminal. And I still have some nagging memories concerning <i>The Adventure of Charles Augustus Milverton</i>, as I am sure that I have previously encountered this plotline and I cannot remember where! Most likely in a more recent book in which someone has borrowed from the master, but I am being driven mad because I cannot recall the source.<br /><br />I am so glad that Doyle brought Holmes back if only because we got <i>The Adventure of the Dancing Men</i> out of the deal. What an excellent story of code-breaking and villain-catching!<br /><br />I hold the author to blame, however, for the idea that men should be cold, intellectual, and detached from society. I think that our society would be much better if more men aspired to be John Watson, rather than Sherlock Holmes!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14253946449187578317" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2435760656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11947035306660453712">NOTE: Original review magically deleted by nefarious internet forces <br/><br/>The sixth installment of the Holmes series, and the third collection of short stories, tells of Holmes' survival from his apparent fate in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4702372.The_Adventure_of_the_Final_Problem__The_Memoirs_of_Sherlock_Holmes___11_' title='The Adventure of the Final Problem (The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes, #11) by Arthur Conan Doyle' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Adventure of the Final Problem</a>. Doyle's writing style has noticably improved since the prior short story collection, and the plot ideas, which had grown quite stale, are generally more creative although there is still a heavy reliance on "someone's past comes back to haunt them." <br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8289147.The_Adventure_of_the_Empty_House__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___1_' title='The Adventure of the Empty House (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #1) by Arthur Conan Doyle' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Adv</a></span>
  <span id="freeText11947035306660453712" style="display:none">NOTE: Original review magically deleted by nefarious internet forces <br /><br />The sixth installment of the Holmes series, and the third collection of short stories, tells of Holmes' survival from his apparent fate in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4702372.The_Adventure_of_the_Final_Problem__The_Memoirs_of_Sherlock_Holmes___11_" title="The Adventure of the Final Problem (The Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes, #11) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of the Final Problem</a>. Doyle's writing style has noticably improved since the prior short story collection, and the plot ideas, which had grown quite stale, are generally more creative although there is still a heavy reliance on "someone's past comes back to haunt them." <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8289147.The_Adventure_of_the_Empty_House__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___1_" title="The Adventure of the Empty House (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #1) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of the Empty House</a> - 3/5 - explains Holmes' return but not much of a mystery to solve<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8319517.The_Adventure_of_the_Norwood_Builder__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___2_" title="The Adventure of the Norwood Builder (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #2) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of the Norwood Builder</a> - 3/5 - surprising twist to this murder<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/790381.The_Adventure_of_the_Dancing_Men__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___3_" title="The Adventure of the Dancing Men (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #3) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of the Dancing Men</a> - 4/5 - past comes back to haunt someone, but the code is fun and makes the old plot more tolerable<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2074730.The_Adventure_of_the_Solitary_Cyclist__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___4_" title="The Adventure of the Solitary Cyclist (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #4) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of the Solitary Cyclist</a> - 4/5 - So look out for those beauties oh yeah<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16072502.The_Adventure_of_the_Priory_School" title="The Adventure of the Priory School by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of the Priory School</a> - 4/5 - longer Holmes story with lots of work for Holmes and Watson to do<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16072504.The_Adventure_of_Black_Peter__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___6_" title="The Adventure of Black Peter (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #6) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of Black Peter</a> - 3/5 - what's that you say? Someone's past has come back to haunt them?<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16072510.The_Adventure_of_Charles_Augustus_Milverton__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___7_" title="The Adventure of Charles Augustus Milverton (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #7) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of Charles Augustus Milverton</a> - 4/5 - Holmes and Watson on the other side of the law!<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9015611.Sherlock_Holmes_and_the_Adventure_of_the_Six_Napoleons__On_the_Case_with_Holmes___Watson___9_" title="Sherlock Holmes and the Adventure of the Six Napoleons (On the Case with Holmes &amp; Watson, #9) by Murray Shaw" rel="nofollow noopener">Sherlock Holmes and the Adventure of the Six Napoleons</a> - 4/5 - Not hard to figure out but fun nevertheless<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8289097.The_Adventure_Of_The_Three_Students__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___9_" title="The Adventure Of The Three Students (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #9) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure Of The Three Students</a> - 4/5 - Holmes and Watson back to school<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16072511.The_Adventure_of_the_Golden_Pince_Nez" title="The Adventure of the Golden Pince-Nez by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of the Golden Pince-Nez</a> - 3/5 - Someone's past comes back to...aw, you know the rest <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16072525.The_Adventure_of_the_Missing_Three_Quarter__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___11_" title="The Adventure of the Missing Three-Quarter (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #11) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of the Missing Three-Quarter</a> - 3/5 - *sigh*...would you believe it? Someone's past comes back to haunt them...<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15848325.Adventure_of_the_Abbey_Grange__The_Return_of_Sherlock_Holmes___12_" title="Adventure of the Abbey Grange (The Return of Sherlock Holmes, #12) by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">Adventure of the Abbey Grange</a> - 4/5 - Case solved! Or is it...? <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22221736.The_Adventure_of_the_Second_Stain" title="The Adventure of the Second Stain by Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">The Adventure of the Second Stain</a> - 4/5 - there must have been a second stain, over there on the grassy knoll! </span>

<div id="review_1933078373" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1933078373" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1933078373?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6193673-the-return-of-sherlock-holmes">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1933078373" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13111274992889835027">After Doyle said he would no longer write about his famous consulting detective, one might reasonably expect the quality of his stories to go down after he returned to them (due to popular demand), but I didn’t find that the case (pun unintended). Yes, some of the elements are a bit overfamiliar by now, but fans expected those, and without them Holmes and Watson are not themselves.<br/><br/>The murders seem more violent (give the people what they want?), but the humorous dialogue (<i>“…so you can put that in</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13111274992889835027" style="display:none">After Doyle said he would no longer write about his famous consulting detective, one might reasonably expect the quality of his stories to go down after he returned to them (due to popular demand), but I didn’t find that the case (pun unintended). Yes, some of the elements are a bit overfamiliar by now, but fans expected those, and without them Holmes and Watson are not themselves.<br /><br />The murders seem more violent (give the people what they want?), but the humorous dialogue (<i>“…so you can put that in your pipe and smoke it, Mr. Busybody Holmes!”</i> and <i>“What did you do, Hopkins, after you made certain that you had made certain of nothing?”</i>); and the nice descriptive writing (<i>…ten miles of man’s handiwork on every side of us, to feel the iron grip of Nature…to the huge elemental forces all London was no more than the molehills that dot the fields.</i>) are still present, along with something new—graphics (drawings mostly) provided to the readers so they can see what Sherlock sees. <br /><br />At the end of this collection, Watson says Sherlock no longer wants his stories to be published now that he has retired to the country. He wants no more publicity and to be left in peace. Poor Doyle.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13111274992889835027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2002859607" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2002859607" />
    <a title="Tristram Shandy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18181298-tristram-shandy"><img alt="Tristram Shandy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589454223p2/18181298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2002859607?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tristram Shandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tristram Shandy" href="/user/show/18181298-tristram-shandy">Tristram Shandy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18181298-tristram-shandy?shelf=crimes-stories">crimes-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18181298-tristram-shandy?shelf=classic-english-literature">classic-english-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2002859607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16073283390775995925"><b>Never Say Die, Watson!</b><br/><br/>In 1901 Arthur Conan Doyle heard about the legend of a ghostly hound haunting the marshes of Dartmoor, and he was so fascinated by this tale that he decided to turn it into a story about an old family curse. Originally, there was no talk of Sherlock Holmes in this context at first, but since it occurred to the writer that he would need the character of a detective in his tale to have the family mystery unravelled, Doyle made up his mind to reactivate Sherlock Holmes, a conc</span>
  <span id="freeText16073283390775995925" style="display:none"><b>Never Say Die, Watson!</b><br /><br />In 1901 Arthur Conan Doyle heard about the legend of a ghostly hound haunting the marshes of Dartmoor, and he was so fascinated by this tale that he decided to turn it into a story about an old family curse. Originally, there was no talk of Sherlock Holmes in this context at first, but since it occurred to the writer that he would need the character of a detective in his tale to have the family mystery unravelled, Doyle made up his mind to reactivate Sherlock Holmes, a conclusion in which the prospect of increasing his royalty from the <i>Strand Magazine</i> might have had some little momentum. However, as readers knew – to their chagrin – Holmes had met the Grim Reaper in his final struggle with Professor Moriarty, and therefore Doyle set the story told in <i>The Hound of the Baskervilles</i> before the fatal duel between the master detective and the master scoundrel. <br /><br />Some time later, though, Doyle gave in to further public demand for new Sherlock Holmes adventures, although before killing off his popular creation in the Reichenbach Falls he was sure that unless he killed Holmes, Holmes would sooner or later kill him. This time, however, it seemed impossible to have all the new cases take place before 1891, and so Doyle did something that is very familiar to watchers of modern soap operas: He undid the death of a character by retrospectively rewriting part of the story. As can be expected of Doyle, he was a little bit more imaginative than simply to claim that Watson had just dreamed Holmes’s death – a solution that would have laid itself open to troubling psychoanalytical interpretations, by the way –; instead, in the first story, <u>The Empty House</u>, we learn what <i>really</i> happened on that fateful day in Switzerland, and also why Holmes had chosen to pass for dead from 1891-94. Giving a credible account of how Holmes could have made his escape from the annoying scrape into which he was put by Moriarty, Doyle had to cheat a little bit by retrospectively altering decisive details given in Watson’s original account of the demise of Sherlock Holmes. All the same, he does it in a way that rather wrests an indulgent smile from his readers than make them bristle, for example here:<br /><br /><blockquote>”’I stood up and examined the rocky wall behind me. In your picturesque account of the matter, which I read with great interest some months later, you assert that the wall was sheer. That was not literally true. A few small footholds presented themselves, and there was some indication of a ledge. […]’”</blockquote><br /><br />Probably, Watson, who might have used his years with Sherlock Holmes to hone his powers of observation, also overlooked the canopied moving stairs that lead from the ledge directly to a smokers’ lounge from which the detective was able to follow the aftermath of the Reichenbach Fall events? Be that as it may, Holmes and Watson are once again reunited and, since Watson’s wife Mary has deplorably died, or conveniently been abducted by aliens, or simply vanished into thin air – our narrator hardly thinks it worthwhile to let us in on that secret –, the two men take up lodgings once again at 221b Baker Street and embark on a series of adventures the first one of which is laying Moriarty’s right hand, Colonel Sebastian Moran, by the heels.<br /><br /><blockquote>”’[…] We will see if my three years of absence have entirely taken away my power to surprise you.’”</blockquote><br /><br />Thus Holmes to Watson in the course of their emotion-laden first encounter after such a long spell of time, and yes! most of the new cases collected in <i>The Return of Sherlock Holmes</i> are highly entertaining and may well surprise the first-time reader, and it is from this collection, as well as from its two predecessors, that most film adaptations draw their material. There are some sparkling highlights among them, such as <u>The Adventure of the Solitary Cyclist</u> or <u>The Adventure of the Priory School</u>, which are well-known to any one even slightly familiar with Sherlock Holmes. At times, however, Doyle also practises the art of recycling his old tricks as when, in <u>The Adventure of the Norwood Builder</u>, Holmes uses a controlled fire to solve his case, and what is good enough to baffle an extraordinary woman like Irene Adler will most certainly do its service to bring down a lesser criminal. The only thing that I found strange about this adventure is how the police could take charred rabbit bones for the remains of a human body – but if I remember correctly, Lestrade was on the case, and so again, it makes perfect sense. In one instance, namely <u>The Adventure of the Six Napoleons</u>, the parallel to an older case is so obvious – you surely remember the Christmas geese, don’t you? – that one may begin to fear that Doyle’s inventiveness was beginning to slacken, but these doubts are soon dispelled.<br /><br />All in all, there are some very effective murder mysteries in this collection, my favourite being <u>The Adventure of the Golden Pince-Nez</u>, where Holmes gets a wonderful opportunity to show his skills of observation, deduction and, above all, setting traps. Other stories, like the one about Holmes’s encounter with the ruthless blackmailer Charles Augustus Milverton, an evil Mr. Pickwick, on the whole put our sleuth into the role of an observer. This story is remarkable nevertheless because it gives us a glimpse on the darker side of Sherlock Holmes – when, seeing the affair as <i>”a sporting duel between this fellow Milverton and [himself]”</i>, he ruthlessly engages himself to Milverton’s maid in order to win her confidence and gain access into her master’s well-guarded house. Watson utters his qualms about Holmes’s procedure but is rebuffed in this way:<br /><br /><blockquote>”’You can’t help it, my dear Watson. You must play your cards as best you can when such a stake is on the table. […]’”</blockquote><br /><br />Surely, such a degree of callous indifference to the feelings of a housemaid displays an unsettling streak of misogyny in Holmes’s character that should incur more censure from Watson. As usual, we also get a lot of hints at cases that didn’t find their way into Watson’s published collection but nevertheless have such an appetizing ring of mystery and oddness about them that we ardently wish someone <i>would</i> spread them before the public eye:<br /><br /><blockquote>”In this memorable year ‘95, a curious and incongruous succession of cases had engaged his attention, ranging from his famous investigation of the sudden death of Cardinal Tosca — an inquiry which was carried out by him at the express desire of His Holiness the Pope — down to his arrest of Wilson, the notorious canary-trainer, which removed a plague-spot from the East End of London.”</blockquote><br /><br />Just imagine what kind of man Wilson, the notorious canary-trainer, might have been, and what the canaries could have been trained at – theft, burglary, impersonation of bigger birds like Christmas geese? Interestingly, Doyle seems to have thought that <i>The Return of Sherlock Holmes</i> should have been his very last foray into the exciting professional life of Sherlock Holmes, because at the beginning of the last story, <u>The Adventure of the Second Stain</u>, Watson tells us that Holmes has by now retired from his work and become a beekeeper on the Sussex Downs and that at his express wish, Watson is to abstain from publishing further cases from his career. Still, we need not despair because, as history would show, Holmes had not done with Doyle yet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16073283390775995925" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16442509774283212333" style="display:none">The men and women of victorian England went about wearing black armbands in mourning the death of fictional Sherlock Holmes after ACD's last story collection, which was meant to be the last. <br />The following Hound of the Baskervilles was not a true SH story insomuch as it was Watson's story. The world needed another collection, apparently. <br />If you read all the Sherlock Homles story collections in order and in a quick succession like I did, you'll tire out somewhat and distinguishing them all becomes hard. However, this collection is consistently good, but the different stories are indistinguishable and forgetable (to me). <br />So read this if you simply enjoy SH. <br /></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781408468258" href="/book/show/11398489-the-return-of-sherlock-holmes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=mysteries-thrillers">mysteries-thrillers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=british-charm">british-charm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=classics">classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer640003537" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12786068373285320093">Sherlock Holmes is back! The first story in this collection, The Adventure of the Empty House, was one of my favorites because it's how Arthur Conan Doyle brought the great detective back from the dead. Doyle had previously killed the character because he was tired of writing about him, but fans were so upset (and publishers offered him more money!) that the author finally relented and wrote more stories.<br/><br/>My other favorite stories in this collection were The Adventure of the Norwood Builder, The </span>
  <span id="freeText12786068373285320093" style="display:none">Sherlock Holmes is back! The first story in this collection, The Adventure of the Empty House, was one of my favorites because it's how Arthur Conan Doyle brought the great detective back from the dead. Doyle had previously killed the character because he was tired of writing about him, but fans were so upset (and publishers offered him more money!) that the author finally relented and wrote more stories.<br /><br />My other favorite stories in this collection were The Adventure of the Norwood Builder, The Adventure of Charles Augustus Milverton, and The Adventure of the Abbey Grange. I especially liked how Sherlock occasionally acts as judge and jury, and his reasons for allowing certain perpetrators to escape because he thinks their actions were justified.<br /><br />After making my way through six books of Sherlock, I have come to appreciate how important the character of Dr. Watson is. He must always be simple-minded and astonished by Holmes' fantastic deductions. Without that friendly reaction, I don't think these stories would have been as endearing. <br /><br />Next up: The Valley of Fear. As Sherlock famously said, "The game is afoot!" <br /><br />Note: I've been listening to all of these Sherlock stories on audio CD read by the marvelous British actor Derek Jacobi. I cannot say enough good things about his narration. I highly recommend them.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12786068373285320093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2119845923" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2119845923" />
    <a title="Katie Lumsden" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8190907-katie-lumsden"><img alt="Katie Lumsden" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565906600p2/8190907.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2119845923?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katie Lumsden" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katie Lumsden" href="/user/show/8190907-katie-lumsden">Katie Lumsden</a>

        rated it

  <span id="freeText13357140315105958240" style="display:none">It was good to spend time with rogues from another century. And this mastermind who has such incredible analytic powers but somehow also has a soft heart for desperate cases. Humanity doesn't change much, but civility sure has. Sherlock Holmes... took me decades to finally read about the illustrious detective, and I saw Benedict Cumberbatch play the role before I came to the original stories, which evidently influenced how I picture the famous sleuth. But regardless, the original cases in their now historical settings somehow remain fresh. I can see now what the fuss is all about, and why there are so many fans and Sherlock Holmes clubs and devotees around the world. I might have become one too had I, like Stephen Fry—who narrates this audiobook to perfection, with little introductions to every volume—discovered him as a wee young thing, filled with curiosity and eager to put my intelligence and obsessive attention to detail to good use.</span>

<div id="review_3188184268" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3188184268" />
    <a title="Stephanie Anze" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27117957-stephanie-anze"><img alt="Stephanie Anze" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604709893p2/27117957.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3188184268?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie Anze" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie Anze" href="/user/show/27117957-stephanie-anze">Stephanie Anze</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27117957-stephanie-anze?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27117957-stephanie-anze?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3188184268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15565234887744137232">"I have heard your name, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, and I am aware of your profession- one of which I by no means approve."<br/>"In that, Doctor, you will find yourself in agreement with every criminal in the country," said my friend, quietly.<br/><br/>Its been three years since Sherlock Holmes died, or so thought his companion, Dr. Watson. One day Watson notices a rather unusual individual who reveals himself to be the famed detective. Sherlock explains how he could not come back until now for certain individuals a</span>
  <span id="freeText15565234887744137232" style="display:none">"I have heard your name, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, and I am aware of your profession- one of which I by no means approve."<br />"In that, Doctor, you will find yourself in agreement with every criminal in the country," said my friend, quietly.<br /><br />Its been three years since Sherlock Holmes died, or so thought his companion, Dr. Watson. One day Watson notices a rather unusual individual who reveals himself to be the famed detective. Sherlock explains how he could not come back until now for certain individuals after him needed to believe he was dead. And Holmes returns in the nick of time as London and its surroundings need him to put criminals away. In a volume of thirteen short stories, Watson chronicles some of the detective's most impressive feats.<br /><br />This has been a most enjoyable and impressive collection of short stories. I have to say, its one of my favorite Holmes collection that I have read so far. Sherlock Holmes has been presumed dead for the past three years. In reality, the detective survived but stayed away for some time for some members of Moriarty's criminal organization were after him. But now he is back and ready to take on the cases that baffle Scotland Yard and other individuals and institutions. This is a good mix of stories as there s variety of crimes, characters, motives, red herrings and impressive reveals. And the interactions between Sherlock and Watson never dissapoint.The quality of the prose is exquisite and each story is fantatically crafted. I wish there had been more. Needless to say, I thorougly enjoyed this book.<br /><br /><i>The Return of Sherlock Holmes</i> was first published as separate short stories in <i>The Strand Magazine</i> from 1903-1904. This volume follows right after Holmes' "death" in <i>The Final Problem</i>, published in 1893. Arthur Conan Doyle found himself under pressure to bring back the famed detective by the public after the unexpected death and I, for one, am glad he did. Its sad that there is a finite amount of Sherlock Holmes books and I myself, is reaching the end (I am reading the Sherlock Holmes books in sequence). What a treat it is to read these books.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15565234887744137232" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
